class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;color:#000000; background: #eeeeee; text-align:center;">Modern period</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912&#8211;1949)">Republic of China</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Province,_Republic_of_China" title="Xinjiang Province, Republic of China">Xinjiang Province</a>)</li>\n<li><a China</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang U.A.R</a>/<a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Production_and_Construction_Corps" title="Xinjiang Production and Construction Corps">Bingtuan</a>)</li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Xinjiang" title="Template:History of Xinjiang"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:History_of_Xinjiang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:History of Xinjiang (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Xinjiang&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Xiongnu</b> <span class="IPA" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[&#597;j&#650;&#769;&#331;.nu&#780;]</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">&#21256;&#22900;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Hsiung-nu</span></i>) were a tribal <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomads" title="Nomads">nomadic peoples</a> who, according to ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">Chinese sources</a>, inhabited the eastern <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">Eurasian Steppe</a> from the 3rd century BC to the late 1st century AD. <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Chinese sources</a> report that <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a>, the supreme leader after 209&#160;BC, founded the Xiongnu Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After their previous overlords, the <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>, migrated into <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> during the 2nd century BC, the Xiongnu became a dominant power on the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> of north-east <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, centred on an area known later as <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>. The Xiongnu were also active in areas now part of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>. Their relations with adjacent Chinese dynasties to the south east were complex, with repeated periods of conflict and intrigue, alternating with exchanges of <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a>, trade, and marriage treaties.\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Sixteen_Kingdoms" title="Sixteen Kingdoms">Sixteen Kingdoms</a> era, they were known as one of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Barbarians" title="Five Barbarians">Five Barbarians</a>.\n</p><p>Attempts to identify the Xiongnu with later groups of the western <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">Eurasian Steppe</a> remain controversial. <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> were concurrently to the west. The identity of the ethnic core of Xiongnu has been a subject of varied hypotheses, because only a few words, mainly titles and personal names, were preserved in the Chinese sources. The name <i>Xiongnu</i> may be <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> with that of the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> or the <i><a href="/wiki/Huna_people" title="Huna people">Huna</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene-5">[5]</a></sup> although this is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaissiere2006_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-vaissiere2006-7">[7]</a></sup> Other linguistic links &#8211; all of them also controversial &#8211; proposed by scholars include <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmatta488_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harmatta488-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey21_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey21-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jankowski26_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowski26-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mongolic_languages" title="Mongolic languages">Mongolic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tumen_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tumen-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hucker_1975_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hucker_1975-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yeniseian_languages" title="Yeniseian languages">Yeniseian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> or multi-ethnic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geng_2005_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geng_2005-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>An early reference to the Xiongnu was by the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> who wrote about the Xiongnu in the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;100</span> BC), drawing a distinct line between the settled <a href="/wiki/Huaxia" title="Huaxia">Huaxia</a> people (Chinese) to the pastoral nomads (Xiongnu), characterizing it as two polar groups in the sense of a civilization versus an uncivilized society: the <a href="/wiki/Hua%E2%80%93Yi_distinction" title="Hua&#8211;Yi distinction">Hua&#8211;Yi distinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Pre-Han sources often classify the Xiongnu as a <a href="/wiki/Five_Barbarians" title="Five Barbarians"><i>Hu</i> people</a>, which was a blanket term for nomadic people in general; it only became an ethnonym for the Xiongnu during the Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ancient China often came in contact with the <a href="/wiki/Xianyun" title="Xianyun">Xianyun</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Xirong" title="Xirong">Xirong</a> nomadic peoples. In later Chinese historiography, some groups of these peoples were believed to be the possible progenitors of the Xiongnu people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cos02-107_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-cos02-107-20">[20]</a></sup> These nomadic people often had repeated military confrontations with the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> and especially the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a>, who often conquered and enslaved the nomads in an expansion drift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cos02-107_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-cos02-107-20">[20]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, the armies from the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(state)" title="Zhao (state)">Zhao</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yan_(state)" title="Yan (state)">Yan</a> states were encroaching and conquering various nomadic territories that were inhabited by the Xiongnu and other Hu peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosm999-892-3_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosm999-892-3-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sinologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Pulleyblank" title="Edwin Pulleyblank">Edwin Pulleyblank</a> argued that the Xiongnu were part of a Xirong group called <a href="/wiki/Yiqu" title="Yiqu">Yiqu</a>, who had lived in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaanbei" title="Shaanbei">Shaanbei</a> and had been influenced by China for centuries, before they were driven out by the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPulleyblank200020_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPulleyblank200020-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Qin%27s_campaign_against_the_Xiongnu" title="Qin\'s campaign against the Xiongnu">Qin\'s campaign against the Xiongnu</a> expanded Qin\'s territory at the expense of the Xiongnu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cos998923964_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-cos998923964-23">[23]</a></sup> In 215 BC, <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> sent General <a href="/wiki/Meng_Tian" title="Meng Tian">Meng Tian</a> to conquer the Xiongnu and drive them from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordos_Loop" title="Ordos Loop">Ordos Loop</a>, which he did later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith71_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith71-24">[24]</a></sup> After the catastrophic defeat at the hands of Meng Tian, the Xiongnu leader <a href="/wiki/Touman" title="Touman">Touman</a> was forced to flee far into the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_Plateau" title="Mongolian Plateau">Mongolian Plateau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith71_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith71-24">[24]</a></sup> The Qin empire became a threat to the Xiongnu, which ultimately led to the reorganization of the many tribes into a confederacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cos998923964_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-cos998923964-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xiongnu_tribes">Xiongnu tribes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Xiongnu tribes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Chubei<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToh200511_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToh200511-25">[25]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Huyan" title="Huyan">Huyan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lan_(tribe)" title="Lan (tribe)">Lan (tribe)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Luandi" title="Luandi">Luandi</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Qiulin" title="Qiulin">Qiulin</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Xubu" title="Xubu">Xubu</a></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_formation">State formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: State formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hsiung-nu-Empire.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hsiung-nu-Empire.png/220px-Hsiung-nu-Empire.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hsiung-nu-Empire.png/330px-Hsiung-nu-Empire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hsiung-nu-Empire.png/440px-Hsiung-nu-Empire.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hsiung-nu-Empire.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Domain and influence of Xiongnu under <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a> around 205 BC</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_200bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Asia_200bc.jpg/220px-Asia_200bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Asia_200bc.jpg/330px-Asia_200bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Asia_200bc.jpg/440px-Asia_200bc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_200bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asia in 200 BC, showing the early Xiongnu state and its neighbors</div></div></div>\n<p>In 209 BC, three years before the founding of Han China, the Xiongnu were brought together in a powerful <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederation</a> under a new <a href="/wiki/Chanyu" title="Chanyu">chanyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a>. This new political unity transformed them into a more formidable state by enabling formation of larger armies and the ability to exercise better strategic coordination. The Xiongnu adopted many of the Chinese agriculture techniques such as slave labor for heavy labor, wore silk like the Chinese, and lived in Chinese-style homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The reason for creating the confederation remains unclear. Suggestions include the need for a stronger state to deal with the Qin unification of China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> that resulted in a loss of the Ordos region at the hands of <a href="/wiki/Meng_Tian" title="Meng Tian">Meng Tian</a> or the political crisis that overtook the Xiongnu in 215 BC when Qin armies evicted them from their pastures on the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After forging internal unity, Modu expanded the empire on all sides. To the north he conquered a number of nomadic peoples, including the <a href="/wiki/Dingling" title="Dingling">Dingling</a> of southern Siberia. He crushed the power of the <a href="/wiki/Donghu_people" title="Donghu people">Donghu people</a> of eastern Mongolia and Manchuria as well as the <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>, where his son, Jizhu, made a <a href="/wiki/Skull_cup" title="Skull cup">skull cup</a> out of the Yuezhi king. Modu also reoccupied all the lands previously taken by the Qin general Meng Tian.\n</p><p>Under Modu\'s leadership, the Xiongnu threatened the Han dynasty, almost causing <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Han" title="Emperor Gaozu of Han">Emperor Gaozu</a>, the first Han emperor, to lose his throne in 200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jerry_Bentley_1993_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jerry_Bentley_1993-29">[29]</a></sup> By the time of Modu\'s death in 174 BC, the Xiongnu had driven the Yuezhi from the Hexi Corridor, killing the Yuezhi king in the process and asserting their presence in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rene-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Xiongnu were recognized as the most prominent of the nomads bordering the Chinese Han empire<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jerry_Bentley_1993_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jerry_Bentley_1993-29">[29]</a></sup> and during early relations between the Xiongnu and the Han, the former held the balance of power. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i>, later quoted in <a href="/wiki/Duan_Chengshi" title="Duan Chengshi">Duan Chengshi</a>\'s ninth century <i><a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Morsels_from_Youyang" title="Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang">Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang</a></i>:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Also, according to the <i>Han shu</i>, Wang Wu (&#29579;&#28879;) and others were sent as envoys to pay a visit to the Xiongnu. According to the customs of the Xiongnu, if the Han envoys did not remove their tallies of authority, and if they did not allow their faces to be tattooed, they could not gain entrance into the yurts. Wang Wu and his company removed their tallies, submitted to tattoo, and thus gained entry. The Shanyu looked upon them very highly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xiongnu_hierarchy">Xiongnu hierarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Xiongnu hierarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chanyu" title="Chanyu">Chanyu</a></div>\n<p>After Modu, later leaders formed a dualistic system of political organisation with the left and right branches of the Xiongnu divided on a regional basis. The <i>chanyu</i> or <i>shanyu</i>, a ruler equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor of China</a>, exercised direct authority over the central territory. <a href="/wiki/Longcheng_District" title="Longcheng District">Longcheng</a> (&#34338;&#22478;), near the <a href="/wiki/Orkhon_inscriptions" title="Orkhon inscriptions">Orkhon inscriptions</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, became the annual meeting place and served as the Xiongnu capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuuu86-384_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuuu86-384-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ruler of the Xiongnu was called the <a href="/wiki/Chanyu" title="Chanyu">Chanyu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bar81-48_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-bar81-48-32">[32]</a></sup> Under him were the <a href="/wiki/Tuqi_King" title="Tuqi King">Tuqi Kings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bar81-48_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-bar81-48-32">[32]</a></sup> The Tuqi King of the Left was normally the heir presumptive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bar81-48_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-bar81-48-32">[32]</a></sup> Next lower in the hierarchy came more officials in pairs of left and right: the <i>guli</i>, the army commanders, the great governors, the <i>dunghu</i> and the <i>gudu</i>. Beneath them came the commanders of detachments of one thousand, of one hundred, and of ten men. This nation of nomads, a people on the march, was organized like an army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Yap,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> apparently describing the early period, places the Chanyu\'s main camp north of <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> with the Tuqi King of the Left holding the area north of Beijing and the Tuqi King of the Right holding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordos_Loop" title="Ordos Loop">Ordos Loop</a> area as far as <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>. Grousset,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> probably describing the situation after the Xiongnu had been driven north, places the Chanyu on the upper <a href="/wiki/Orkhon_River" title="Orkhon River">Orkhon River</a> near where <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> would later establish his capital of <a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">Karakorum</a>. The Tuqi King of the Left lived in the east, probably on the high <a href="/wiki/Kherlen_River" title="Kherlen River">Kherlen River</a>. The Tuqi King of the Right lived in the west, perhaps near present-day <a href="/wiki/Uliastai" title="Uliastai">Uliastai</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Khangai_Mountains" title="Khangai Mountains">Khangai Mountains</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_marriage_treaty_system">The marriage treaty system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The marriage treaty system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mounted_archer,_China,_Han_dynasty,_c._50_BC_-_50_AD,_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3788" data-file-width="3155" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mounted_archer%2C_China%2C_Han_dynasty%2C_c._50_BC_-_50_AD%2C_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG/200px-Mounted_archer%2C_China%2C_Han_dynasty%2C_c._50_BC_-_50_AD%2C_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mounted_archer%2C_China%2C_Han_dynasty%2C_c._50_BC_-_50_AD%2C_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG/300px-Mounted_archer%2C_China%2C_Han_dynasty%2C_c._50_BC_-_50_AD%2C_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mounted_archer%2C_China%2C_Han_dynasty%2C_c._50_BC_-_50_AD%2C_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG/400px-Mounted_archer%2C_China%2C_Han_dynasty%2C_c._50_BC_-_50_AD%2C_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mounted_archer,_China,_Han_dynasty,_c._50_BC_-_50_AD,_glazed_earthenware_-_Royal_Ontario_Museum_-_DSC04035.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Han Chinese glazed ceramic figurine of a mounted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_archer" title="Horse archer">horse archer</a>, 50 BC to 50 AD, late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han_Dynasty" title="Western Han Dynasty">Western</a> or early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_Dynasty" title="Eastern Han Dynasty">Eastern Han Dynasty</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the winter of 200 BC, following a siege of <a href="/wiki/Taiyuan" title="Taiyuan">Taiyuan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Han" title="Emperor Gaozu of Han">Emperor Gaozu of Han</a> personally led a military campaign against Modun. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baideng" title="Battle of Baideng">Battle of Baideng</a>, he was ambushed reputedly by 300,000 elite Xiongnu cavalry. The emperor was cut off from supplies and reinforcements for seven days, only narrowly escaping capture.\n</p><p>The Han Chinese sent princesses to marry Xiongnu leaders in their efforts to stop the border raids. Along with arranged marriages, the Han sent gifts to bribe the Xiongnu to stop attacking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> After the defeat at Pingcheng, the Han emperor abandoned a military solution to the Xiongnu threat. Instead, in 198<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BC, the courtier <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Liu_Jing_(Han_dynasty)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Liu Jing (Han dynasty) (page does not exist)">Liu Jing</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;[<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%8A%89%E6%95%AC_(%E8%A5%BF%E6%BC%A2)" title="zh:&#21129;&#25964; (&#35199;&#28450;)">zh</a>]</span> was dispatched for negotiations. The peace settlement eventually reached between the parties included a Han princess given in marriage to the <i>chanyu</i> (called <i><a href="/wiki/Heqin" title="Heqin">heqin</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">&#21644;&#35242;</span>; literally: "harmonious kinship"); periodic gifts to the Xiongnu of <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">distilled beverages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>; equal status between the states; and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a> as mutual border.\n</p><p>This first treaty set the pattern for relations between the Han and the Xiongnu for sixty years. Up to 135&#160;BC, the treaty was renewed nine times, each time with an increase in the "gifts". In 192&#160;BC, Modun even asked for the hand of Emperor Gao\'s widow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_L%C3%BC_Zhi" title="Empress L&#252; Zhi">Empress L&#252; Zhi</a>. His son and successor, the energetic Jiyu, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laoshang_Chanyu" title="Laoshang Chanyu">Laoshang Chanyu</a>, continued his father\'s expansionist policies. Laoshang succeeded in negotiating with <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Han" title="Emperor Wen of Han">Emperor Wen</a> terms for the maintenance of a large scale government sponsored market system.\n</p><p>While the Xiongnu benefited handsomely, from the Chinese perspective marriage treaties were costly, humiliating, and ineffective. Laoshang showed that he did not take the peace treaty seriously. On one occasion his scouts penetrated to a point near <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>. In 166 BC he personally led 140,000 cavalry to invade Anding, reaching as far as the imperial retreat at Yong. In 158 BC, his successor sent 30,000 cavalry to attack <a href="/wiki/Shangdang" title="Shangdang">Shangdang</a> and another 30,000 to <a href="/wiki/Datong" title="Datong">Yunzhong</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The Xiongnu also <a href="/wiki/Heqin#Xiongnu" title="Heqin">practiced marriage alliances with Han dynasty officers and officials</a> who defected to their side. The older sister of the Chanyu (the Xiongnu ruler) was married to the Xiongnu General <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Xin" title="Zhao Xin">Zhao Xin</a>, the Marquis of Xi who was serving the Han dynasty. The daughter of the Chanyu was married to the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> General <a href="/wiki/Li_Ling" title="Li Ling">Li Ling</a> after he surrendered and defected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimaWatson1993_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-SimaWatson1993-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakeman1985_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wakeman1985-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yenisei_Kirghiz" title="Yenisei Kirghiz">Yenisei Kirghiz</a> Khagans claimed descent from Li Ling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>  Another Han Chinese General who defected to the Xiongnu was <a href="/wiki/Li_Guangli" title="Li Guangli">Li Guangli</a> who also married a daughter of the Chanyu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the Eastern Jin dynasty ended the Xianbei <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a> <a href="/wiki/Heqin#Northern_Wei" title="Heqin">received</a> the Han Chinese <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265&#8211;420)">Jin prince</a> Sima Chuzhi <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%8F%B8%E9%A6%AC%E6%A5%9A%E4%B9%8B" title="zh:&#21496;&#39340;&#26970;&#20043;">&#21496;&#39340;&#26970;&#20043;</a> as a refugee. A Northern Wei Xianbei Princess married Sima Chuzhi, giving birth to Sima Jinlong <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%8F%B8%E9%A6%AC%E9%87%91%E9%BE%8D" title="zh:&#21496;&#39340;&#37329;&#40845;">&#21496;&#39340;&#37329;&#40845;</a>. <a href="/wiki/Northern_Liang" title="Northern Liang">Northern Liang</a> Xiongnu King <a href="/wiki/Juqu_Mujian" title="Juqu Mujian">Juqu Mujian</a>\'s daughter married Sima Jinlong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Han_dynasty">War with Han dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: War with Han dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han&#8211;Xiongnu War">Han&#8211;Xiongnu War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Han_Civilisation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="880" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Han_Civilisation.png/250px-Han_Civilisation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Han_Civilisation.png/375px-Han_Civilisation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Han_Civilisation.png/500px-Han_Civilisation.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Han_Civilisation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> world order in AD 2.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Han dynasty made preparations for war when the Han Emperor Wu dispatched the explorer <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a> to explore the mysterious kingdoms to the west and to form an alliance with the Yuezhi people in order to combat the Xiongnu. During this time Zhang married a Xiongnu wife, who bore him a son, and gained the trust of the Xiongnu leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayor2014_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor2014-51">[51]</a></sup> While Zhang Qian did not succeed in this mission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene2-52">[52]</a></sup> his reports of the west provided even greater incentive to counter the Xiongnu hold on westward routes out of China, and the Chinese prepared to mount a large scale attack using the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Silk_Road" title="Northern Silk Road">Northern Silk Road</a> to move men and material.\n</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han China</a> was making preparations for a military confrontation from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Han" title="Emperor Wen of Han">Emperor Wen</a>, the break did not come until 133 BC, following <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mayi" title="Battle of Mayi">an abortive trap to ambush the <i>chanyu</i> at Mayi</a>. By that point the empire was consolidated politically, militarily and economically, and was led by an adventurous pro-war faction at court. In that year, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han_China" title="Emperor Wu of Han China">Emperor Wu</a> reversed the decision he had made the year before to renew the peace treaty.\n</p><p>Full-scale war broke out in autumn 129 BC, when 40,000 Chinese <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> made a surprise attack on the Xiongnu at the border markets. In 127 BC, the Han general <a href="/wiki/Wei_Qing" title="Wei Qing">Wei Qing</a> retook the Ordos. In 121 BC, the Xiongnu suffered another setback when <a href="/wiki/Huo_Qubing" title="Huo Qubing">Huo Qubing</a> led a force of light cavalry westward out of Longxi and within six days fought his way through five Xiongnu kingdoms. The Xiongnu Hunye king was forced to surrender with 40,000 men. In 119 BC both Huo and Wei, each leading 50,000 cavalrymen and 100,000 footsoldiers (in order to keep up with the mobility of the Xiongnu, many of the non-cavalry Han soldiers were mobile infantrymen who traveled on horseback but fought on foot), and advancing along different routes, forced the <i>chanyu</i> and his court to flee north of the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1974_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1974-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2014)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Major logistical difficulties limited the duration and long-term continuation of these campaigns. According to the analysis of Yan You (&#22196;&#23588;), the difficulties were twofold. Firstly there was the problem of supplying food across long distances.  Secondly, the weather in the northern Xiongnu lands was difficult for Han soldiers, who could never carry enough fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[note 1]</a></sup> According to official reports, the Xiongnu lost 80,000 to 90,000 men, and out of the 140,000 horses the Han forces had brought into the desert, fewer than 30,000 returned to China.\n</p><p>As a result of these battles, the Chinese controlled the strategic region from the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_Desert" title="Ordos Desert">Ordos</a> and Gansu corridor to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lop_Nor" title="Lop Nor">Lop Nor</a>. They succeeded in separating the Xiongnu from the <a href="/wiki/Qiang_people" title="Qiang people">Qiang peoples</a> to the south, and also gained direct access to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a>. Because of strong Chinese control over the Xiongnu, the Xiongnu became unstable and were no longer a threat to the Han Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_001ad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Asia_001ad.jpg/300px-Asia_001ad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Asia_001ad.jpg/450px-Asia_001ad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Asia_001ad.jpg/600px-Asia_001ad.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_001ad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Xiongnu among other people in Asia around 1 AD.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a>, Protector General (&#37117;&#35703;; <i>Duhu</i>) of the Han dynasty, embarked with an army of 70,000 men in a campaign against the Xiongnu insurgents who were harassing the trade route now known as the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.  His successful military campaign saw the subjugation of one Xiongnu tribe after another. Ban Chao also sent an envoy named <a href="/wiki/Gan_Ying" title="Gan Ying">Gan Ying</a> to <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a> (Rome).  Ban Chao was created the Marquess of Dingyuan (&#23450;&#36960;&#20399;, i.e., "the Marquess who stabilized faraway places") for his services to the Han Empire and returned to the capital <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> at the age of 70 years and died there in the year 102.  Following his death, the power of the Xiongnu in the Western Regions increased again, and the emperors of subsequent dynasties were never again able to reach so far to the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Xiongnu_Civil_War_.2860.E2.80.9353_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Xiongnu_Civil_War_(60&#8211;53_BC)">Xiongnu Civil War (60&#8211;53 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Xiongnu Civil War (60&#8211;53 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When a Chanyu died, power could pass to his younger brother if his son was not of age. This system, which can be compared to Gaelic <a href="/wiki/Tanistry" title="Tanistry">tanistry</a>, normally kept  an adult male on the throne, but could cause trouble in later generations when there were several lineages that might claim the throne.  When the 12th Chanyu died in 60 BC, power was taken by Woyanqudi, a grandson of the 12th Chanyu\'s cousin. Being something of a usurper, he tried to put his own men in power, which only increased the number of his enemies. The 12th Chanyu\'s son fled east and, in 58 BC, revolted. Few would support Woyanqudi and he was driven to suicide, leaving the rebel son, Huhanye, as the 14th Chanyu. The Woyanqudi faction then set up his brother, Tuqi, as Chanyu (58 BC). In 57 BC three more men declared themselves Chanyu. Two dropped their claims in favor of the third who was defeated by Tuqi in that year and surrendered to Huhanye the following year. In 56 BC Tuqi was defeated by Huhanye and committed suicide, but two more claimants appeared: Runzhen and Huhanye\'s elder brother <a href="/wiki/Zhizhi_Chanyu" title="Zhizhi Chanyu">Zhizhi Chanyu</a>. Runzhen was killed by Zhizhi in 54 BC, leaving only Zhizhi and Huhanye. Zhizhi grew in power, and, in 53 BC, Huhanye moved south and submitted to the Chinese. Huhanye used Chinese support to weaken Zhizhi, who gradually moved west. In 49 BC, a brother to Tuqi set himself up as Chanyu and was killed by Zhizhi. In 36 BC, Zhizhi was killed by a Chinese army while trying to establish a new kingdom in the far west near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Balkhash" title="Lake Balkhash">Lake Balkhash</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributary_relations_with_the_Han">Tributary relations with the Han</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tributary relations with the Han">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="927" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg/200px-Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg/300px-Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg/400px-Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_seal_of_a_Xiongnu_chief.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze seal says "To <b>Han</b> obedient, friendly and loyal chief of Xiongnu of <b>Han</b> (<b>&#28450;</b>&#21256;&#22900;&#27512;&#32681;&#35242;<b>&#28450;</b>&#38263;)"&#65294;Bronze seal conferred by the Eastern Han government on a Xiongnu chief.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 53 BC <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Huhanye&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Huhanye (page does not exist)">Huhanye</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;[<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%91%BC%E9%9F%93%E9%82%AA%E5%96%AE%E4%BA%8E" title="zh:&#21628;&#38867;&#37034;&#21934;&#20110;">zh</a>]</span> (&#21628;&#38867;&#37034;) decided to enter into tributary relations with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_China" title="Han China">Han China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene1-58">[57]</a></sup> The original terms insisted on by the Han court were that, first, the <i>chanyu</i> or his representatives should come to the capital to pay homage; secondly, the <i>chanyu</i> should send a hostage prince; and thirdly, the <i>chanyu</i> should present tribute to the Han emperor. The political status of the Xiongnu in the Chinese world order was reduced from that of a "brotherly state" to that of an "outer vassal" (&#22806;&#33251;). During this period, however, the Xiongnu maintained political sovereignty and full territorial integrity. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a> continued to serve as the line of demarcation between Han and Xiongnu.\n</p><p>Huhanye sent his son, the "wise king of the right" Shuloujutang, to the Han court as hostage. In 51 BC he personally visited Chang\'an to pay homage to the emperor on the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Lunar New Year</a>. In the same year, another envoy Qijushan (&#31293;&#23621;&#29414;) was received at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet_Spring_Palace" title="Sweet Spring Palace">Sweet Spring Palace</a> in the north west of modern <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbank_&amp;_T&#234;ng1941_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbank_&amp;_T&#234;ng1941-59">[58]</a></sup> On the financial side, Huhanye was amply rewarded in large quantities of gold, cash, clothes, silk, horses and grain for his participation. Huhanye made two further homage trips, in 49 BC and 33 BC; with each one the imperial gifts were increased. On the last trip, Huhanye took the opportunity to ask to be allowed to become an imperial son-in-law. As a sign of the decline in the political status of the Xiongnu, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Yuan_of_Han" title="Emperor Yuan of Han">Emperor Yuan</a> refused, giving him instead five ladies-in-waiting. One of them was <a href="/wiki/Wang_Zhaojun" title="Wang Zhaojun">Wang Zhaojun</a>, famed in Chinese folklore as one of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Beauties" title="Four Beauties">Four Beauties</a>.\n</p><p>When Zhizhi learned of his brother\'s submission, he also sent a son to the Han court as hostage in 53&#160;BC. Then twice, in 51 BC and 50 BC, he sent envoys to the Han court with tribute. But having failed to pay homage personally, he was never admitted to the tributary system. In 36 BC, a junior officer named <a href="/wiki/Chen_Tang" title="Chen Tang">Chen Tang</a>, with the help of Gan Yanshou, protector-general of the Western Regions, assembled an expeditionary force that defeated him at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zhizhi" title="Battle of Zhizhi">Battle of Zhizhi</a> and sent his head as a trophy to Chang\'an.\n</p><p>Tributary relations were discontinued during the reign of Huduershi (18 AD&#8211;48), corresponding to the political upheavals of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xin_Dynasty" title="Xin Dynasty">Xin Dynasty</a> in China. The Xiongnu took the opportunity to regain control of the western regions, as well as neighbouring peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Wuhuan" title="Wuhuan">Wuhuan</a>. In 24 AD, Hudershi even talked about reversing the tributary system.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Xiongnu_and_Northern_Xiongnu">Southern Xiongnu and Northern Xiongnu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Southern Xiongnu and Northern Xiongnu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:401px;max-width:401px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sichuan,_han_orientali,_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta,_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._02.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="223"
