//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._02.JPG/260px-Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._02.JPG 2x" width="130"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:265px;max-width:265px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sichuan,_han_orientali,_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta,_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2313" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG/263px-Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG/395px-Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG/526px-Sichuan%2C_han_orientali%2C_cavallo_con_ciuffo_e_criniera_corta%2C_seconda_met%C3%A0_II-inizio_III_sec._03.JPG 2x" width="263"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> Chinese glazed ceramic statue of a horse with <a href="/wiki/Bridle" title="Bridle">bridle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halter" title="Halter">halter</a> headgear, from <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, late 2nd century to early 3rd century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>The Xiongnu\'s new power was met with a policy of appeasement by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Guangwu_of_Han" title="Emperor Guangwu of Han">Emperor Guangwu</a>. At the height of his power, Huduershi even compared himself to his illustrious ancestor, Modu. Due to growing regionalism among the Xiongnu, however, Huduershi was never able to establish unquestioned authority. In contravention of a principle of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraternal_succession" title="Fraternal succession">fraternal succession</a> established by Huhanye, Huduershi designated his son Punu as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heir-apparent" title="Heir-apparent">heir-apparent</a>. However, as the eldest son of the preceding <i>chanyu</i>, Bi (Pi) &#8211; the Rizhu King of the Right &#8211; had a more legitimate claim. Consequently, Bi refused to attend the annual meeting at the <i>chanyu\'</i>s court. Nevertheless, in 46 AD, Punu ascended the throne.\n</p><p>In 48 AD, a confederation of eight Xiongnu tribes in Bi\'s power base in the south, with a military force totalling 40,000 to 50,000 men, seceded from Punu\'s kingdom and acclaimed Bi as <i>chanyu</i>. This kingdom became known as the <b>Southern Xiongnu</b>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Northern_Xiongnu">The Northern Xiongnu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Northern Xiongnu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Chanyu_(1st_century)" title="Northern Chanyu (1st century)">Northern Chanyu (1st century)</a></div>\n<p>The rump kingdom under Punu, around the <a href="/wiki/Orkhon_Valley" title="Orkhon Valley">Orkhon</a> (modern north central Mongolia) became known as the <b>Northern Xiongnu</b>. Punu, who became known as the Northern <i>Chanyu</i>, began to put military pressure on the Southern Xiongnu.\n</p><p>In 49 AD, Tsi Yung, a Han governor of <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong</a>, allied with the <a href="/wiki/Wuhuan" title="Wuhuan">Wuhuan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a>, attacked the Northern Xiongnu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene0_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene0-60">[59]</a></sup>\nThe Northern Xiongnu suffered two major defeats: one at the hands of the Xianbei in 85 AD, and by the Han during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ikh_Bayan" title="Battle of Ikh Bayan">Battle of Ikh Bayan</a>, in 89 AD.  The northern <i>chanyu</i> fled to the north-west with his subjects.\n</p><p>In about 155 AD, the Northern Xiongnu were decisively "crushed and subjugated" by the <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene100_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene100-61">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_200ad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1209" data-file-width="1910" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Asia_200ad.jpg/250px-Asia_200ad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Asia_200ad.jpg/375px-Asia_200ad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Asia_200ad.jpg/500px-Asia_200ad.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_200ad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southern and Northern Xiongnu in 200 AD, before the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Southern_Xiongnu">The Southern Xiongnu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Southern Xiongnu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Coincidentally, the Southern Xiongnu were plagued by natural disasters and misfortunes &#8211; in addition to the threat posed by Punu. Consequently, in 50 AD, the Southern Xiongnu submitted to tributary relations with Han China. The system of tribute was considerably tightened by the Han, to keep the Southern Xiongnu under control. The <i>chanyu</i> was ordered to establish his court in the Meiji district of Xihe commandery and the Southern Xiongnu were resettled in eight frontier commanderies. At the same time, large numbers of Chinese were also resettled in these commanderies, in mixed Han-Xiongnu settlements.\nEconomically, the Southern Xiongnu became reliant on trade with the Han.\n</p><p>Tensions were evident between Han settlers and practitioners of the nomadic way of life. Thus, in 94, Anguo Chanyu joined forces with newly subjugated Xiongnu from the north and started a large scale rebellion against the Han.\n</p><p>During the late 2nd century AD, the southern Xiongnu were drawn into the rebellions then plaguing the Han court. In 188, the <i>chanyu</i> was murdered by some of his own subjects for agreeing to send troops to help the Han suppress a rebellion in <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a> &#8211; many of the Xiongnu feared that it would set a precedent for unending military service to the Han court. The murdered <i>chanyu\'s</i> son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yufuluo" title="Yufuluo">Yufuluo</a>, entitled Chizhisizhu (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25345;&#33267;&#23608;&#36880;&#20399;</span>), succeeded him, but was then overthrown by the same rebellious faction in 189. He travelled to <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> (the Han capital) to seek aid from the Han court, but at this time the Han court was in disorder from the clash between Grand General <a href="/wiki/He_Jin" title="He Jin">He Jin</a> and the eunuchs, and the intervention of the warlord <a href="/wiki/Dong_Zhuo" title="Dong Zhuo">Dong Zhuo</a>. The <i>chanyu</i> had no choice but to settle down with his followers in <a href="/wiki/Linfen" title="Linfen">Pingyang</a>, a city in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>. In 195, he died and was succeeded as <i>chanyu</i> by his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huchuquan_Chanyu" title="Huchuquan Chanyu">Huchuquan Chanyu</a>.\n</p><p>In 215&#8211;216 AD, the warlord-statesman <a href="/wiki/Cao_Cao" title="Cao Cao">Cao Cao</a> detained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huchuquan_Chanyu" title="Huchuquan Chanyu">Huchuquan Chanyu</a> in the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ye,_China" title="Ye, China">Ye</a>, and divided his followers in Shanxi into five divisions: left, right, south, north, and centre. This was aimed at preventing the exiled Xiongnu in Shanxi from engaging in rebellion, and also allowed Cao Cao to use the Xiongnu as auxiliaries in his cavalry.\n</p><p>Later the Xiongnu aristocracy in Shanxi changed their surname from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luanti" title="Luanti">Luanti</a> to Liu for prestige reasons, claiming that they were related to the Han imperial clan through the old intermarriage policy.\nAfter Huchuquan, the Southern Xiongnu were partitioned into five local tribes.  Each local chief was under the "surveillance of a chinese resident", while the shanyu was in "semicaptivity at the imperial court."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene111_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene111-62">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Xiongnu_states">Later Xiongnu states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Later Xiongnu states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span id="Former_Zhao_state_.28304.E2.80.93329.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Zhao_state_(304&#8211;329)">Former Zhao state (304&#8211;329)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Former Zhao state (304&#8211;329)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Former_Zhao" title="Former Zhao">Former Zhao</a></div>\n<dl><dt>Han Zhao dynasty (304&#8211;318)</dt></dl>\n<p>In 304, <a href="/wiki/Liu_Yuan_(Han_Zhao)" title="Liu Yuan (Han Zhao)">Liu Yuan</a> became Chanyu of the Five Hordes.  In 308, declared himself emperor and founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Zhao" title="Han Zhao">Han Zhao</a> Dynasty.  In 311, his son and  successor <a href="/wiki/Liu_Cong_(Han_Zhao)" title="Liu Cong (Han Zhao)">Liu Cong</a> captured <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, and with it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Huai_of_Jin_China" title="Emperor Huai of Jin China">Emperor Huai of Jin China</a>.\n</p><p>In 316, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Min_of_Jin_China" title="Emperor Min of Jin China">Emperor Min of Jin China</a> was captured in <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>.  Both emperors were humiliated as cupbearers in <a href="/wiki/Linfen" title="Linfen">Linfen</a> before being executed in 313 and 318.\n</p><p>North China came under Xiongnu rule while the remnants of the Jin dynasty survived in the south at <a href="/wiki/Jiankang" title="Jiankang">Jiankang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene1011_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene1011-63">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>The reign of Liu Yao (318&#8211;329)</dt></dl>\n<p>In 318, after suppressing a coup by a powerful minister in the Xiongnu-Han court, in which the emperor and a large proportion of the aristocracy were massacred), the Xiongnu prince <a href="/wiki/Liu_Yao" title="Liu Yao">Liu Yao</a> moved the Xiongnu-Han capital from Pingyang to Chang\'an and renamed the dynasty as Zhao (Liu Yuan had declared the empire\'s name Han to create a linkage with Han Dynasty&#8212;to which he claimed he was a descendant, through a princess, but Liu Yao felt that it was time to end the linkage with Han and explicitly restore the linkage to the great Xiongnu chanyu <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Maodun</a>, and therefore decided to change the name of the state. (However, this was not a break from Liu Yuan, as he continued to honor Liu Yuan and Liu Cong posthumously; it is hence known to historians collectively as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Zhao" title="Han Zhao">Han Zhao</a>).\n</p><p>However, the eastern part of north China came under the control of a rebel Xiongnu-Han general of <a href="/wiki/Jie_people" title="Jie people">Jie</a> ancestry named <a href="/wiki/Shi_Le" title="Shi Le">Shi Le</a>. Liu Yao and Shi Le fought a long war until 329, when Liu Yao was captured in battle and executed. Chang\'an fell to Shi Le soon after, and the Xiongnu dynasty was wiped out. North China was ruled by Shi Le\'s <a href="/wiki/Later_Zhao" title="Later Zhao">Later Zhao</a> dynasty for the next 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene00_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene00-64">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the "Liu" Xiongnu remained active in the north for at least another century.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Tiefu_and_Xia_.28260.E2.80.93431.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tiefu_and_Xia_(260&#8211;431)">Tiefu and Xia (260&#8211;431)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Tiefu and Xia (260&#8211;431)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiefu" title="Tiefu">Tiefu</a> branch of the Xiongnu gained control of the Inner Mongolian region in the 10 years between the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Tuoba" title="Tuoba">Tuoba</a> <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> <a href="/wiki/Dai_(Sixteen_Kingdoms)" title="Dai (Sixteen Kingdoms)">state of Dai</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Former_Qin" title="Former Qin">Former Qin</a> empire in 376, and its restoration in 386 as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a>. After 386, the Tiefu were gradually destroyed by or surrendered to the Tuoba, with the submitting Tiefu becoming known as the Dugu. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liu_Bobo" title="Liu Bobo">Liu Bobo</a>, a surviving prince of the Tiefu fled to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordos_Loop" title="Ordos Loop">Ordos Loop</a>, where he founded a state called the Xia (thus named because of the Xiongnu\'s supposed ancestry from the Xia dynasty) and changed his surname to Helian (&#36203;&#36899;). The Helian-Xia state was conquered by the Northern Wei in 428&#8211;31, and the Xiongnu thenceforth effectively ceased to play a major role in Chinese history, assimilating into the Xianbei and Han ethnicities.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tongwancheng" title="Tongwancheng">Tongwancheng</a> (meaning "Unite All Nations") was the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Xia_(Sixteen_Kingdoms)" title="Xia (Sixteen Kingdoms)">Xia (Sixteen Kingdoms)</a>, whose rulers claimed descent from Modu Chanyu.\n</p><p>The ruined city was discovered in 1996<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> and the State Council designated it as a cultural relic under top state protection. The repair of the Yong\'an Platform, where <a href="/wiki/Helian_Bobo" title="Helian Bobo">Helian Bobo</a>, emperor of the Da Xia regime, reviewed parading troops, has been finished and restoration on the 31-meter-tall turret follows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2014)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Juqu_and_Northern_Liang_.28401.E2.80.93460.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Juqu_and_Northern_Liang_(401&#8211;460)">Juqu and Northern Liang (401&#8211;460)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Juqu and Northern Liang (401&#8211;460)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Juqu were a branch of the Xiongnu. Their leader <a href="/wiki/Juqu_Mengxun" title="Juqu Mengxun">Juqu Mengxun</a> took over the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Liang" title="Northern Liang">Northern Liang</a> by overthrowing the former puppet ruler <a href="/wiki/Duan_Ye" title="Duan Ye">Duan Ye</a>. By 439, the Juqu power was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a>. Their remnants were then settled in the city of <a href="/wiki/Gaochang" title="Gaochang">Gaochang</a> before being destroyed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rouran" title="Rouran">Rouran</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">Interpretation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Interpretation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Barfield<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> attempted to interpret Xiongnu history as well as narrate it. He made the following points: The Xiongnu confederation was unusually long-lived for a steppe empire. The purpose of raiding China was not simply for goods, but to force the Chinese to pay regular tribute. The power of the Xiongnu ruler was based on his control of Chinese tribute which he used to reward his supporters. The Han and Xiongnu empires rose at the same time because the Xiongnu state depended on Chinese tribute.  A major Xiongnu weakness was the custom of lateral succession. If a dead ruler\'s son was not old enough to take command, power passed to the late ruler\'s brother. This worked in the first generation but could lead to civil war in the second generation. The first time this happened, in 60 BC, the weaker party adopted what Barfield calls the \'inner frontier strategy.\'  They moved south and submitted to China and then used Chinese resources to defeat the Northern Xiongnu and re-establish the empire.  The second time this happened, about 47 AD, the strategy failed. The southern ruler was unable to defeat the northern ruler and the Xiongnu remained divided.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_regarding_ethnolinguistic_identity">Theories regarding ethnolinguistic identity<span id="Language"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Theories regarding ethnolinguistic identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin: 10px;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Pronunciation of &#21256;<br/> <small>Source: <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070929174528/http://starling.rinet.ru/cgi-bin/response.cgi?root=config&amp;morpho=0&amp;basename=%5Cdata%5Cchina%5Cbigchina&amp;first=1&amp;text_character=%E5%8C%88" rel="nofollow">http://starling.rinet.ru</a></small>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Preclassic <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a>:</td>\n<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">s&#331;o&#331;</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Classic Old Chinese:</td>\n<td><small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[&#331;&#778;o&#331;]</a></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Postclassic Old Chinese:</td>\n<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ho&#331;</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>:</td>\n<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">x&#246;u&#331;</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin">Modern Mandarin</a>:</td>\n<td><span class="IPA" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[&#597;j&#650;&#769;&#331;]</a></span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><p>\n(See <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_East_Asians" title="Genetic history of East Asians">Genetic history of East Asians</a>)</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_300ad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1903" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/East-Hem_300ad.jpg/250px-East-Hem_300ad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/East-Hem_300ad.jpg/375px-East-Hem_300ad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/East-Hem_300ad.jpg/500px-East-Hem_300ad.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_300ad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Location of Xiongnu and other steppe nations in 300 AD.</div></div></div>\n<p>The sound of the first Chinese character (&#21256;) has been reconstructed as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/q&#688;o&#331;/</span> in Old Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> The Chinese name for the Xiongnu was a <a href="/wiki/Graphic_pejoratives_in_written_Chinese" title="Graphic pejoratives in written Chinese">pejorative term in itself</a>, as the characters have the meaning of "fierce slave".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuuu86-384_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-yuuu86-384-31">[31]</a></sup> The Chinese characters are pronounced as <i>Xi&#333;ngn&#250;</i> <span class="IPA" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[&#597;j&#650;&#769;&#331;nu&#780;]</a></span> in modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Huns">Huns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Huns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_Huns" title="Origin of the Huns">Origin of the Huns</a></div>\n<p>The supposed Old Chinese sound of the first character (&#21256;) has a possible similarity with the name "<a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Hun</a>" in European languages. The second character (&#22900;) appears to have no parallel in Western terminology. Whether the similarity is evidence of kinship or mere coincidence is hard to tell. It could lend credence to the theory that the Huns were in fact descendants of the Northern Xiongnu who migrated westward, or that the Huns were using a name borrowed from the Northern Xiongnu, or that these Xiongnu made up part of the Hun confederation.\n</p><p>The Xiongnu-Hun hypothesis originated with the 18th-century French historian <a href="/wiki/Joseph_de_Guignes" title="Joseph de Guignes">Joseph de Guignes</a>, who noticed that ancient Chinese scholars had referred to members of tribes associated with the Xiongnu by names similar to "Hun", albeit with varying Chinese characters. <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_de_la_Vaissi%C3%A8re" title="&#201;tienne de la Vaissi&#232;re">&#201;tienne de la Vaissi&#232;re</a> has shown that, in the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_alphabet" title="Sogdian alphabet">Sogdian script</a> used in the so-called "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdian_Ancient_Letters&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sogdian Ancient Letters (page does not exist)">Sogdian Ancient Letters</a>", both the Xiongnu and Huns were referred to as &#947;wn (<i>xwn</i>), indicating that the two were synonymous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> Although the theory that the Xiongnu were precursors of the Huns known later in Europe is now accepted by many scholars, it has yet to become a consensus view. The identification with the Huns may be either incorrect or an oversimplification (as would appear to be the case with a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Mongols" title="Proto-Mongols">proto-Mongol</a> people, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rouran" title="Rouran">Rouran</a>, who have sometimes been linked to the <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Avars" title="Pannonian Avars">Avars of Central Europe</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iranian_theories">Iranian theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Iranian theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Harold_Walter_Bailey" title="Harold Walter Bailey">Harold Walter Bailey</a> proposed an <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> origin of the Xiongnu, recognizing all the earliest Xiongnu names of the 2nd century BC as being of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a> type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey21_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey21-9">[9]</a></sup> This theory is supported by <a href="/wiki/Turkology" title="Turkology">turkologist</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://pl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henryk_Jankowski_(turkolog)" title="pl:Henryk Jankowski (turkolog)">Henryk Jankowski</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jankowski26_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowski26-10">[10]</a></sup> Central Asian scholar <a href="/wiki/Christopher_I._Beckwith" title="Christopher I. Beckwith">Christopher I. Beckwith</a> notes that the Xiongnu name could be a cognate of <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdia</a>, corresponding to a name for <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Northern Iranians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith71_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith71-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith405_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith405-71">[70]</a></sup> According to Beckwith the Xiongnu could have contained a leading Iranian component when they started out, but more likely they had earlier been subjects of an Iranian people and learned from them the Iranian nomadic model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith71_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith71-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>-published <i>History of Civilizations of Central Asia</i>, its editor <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Harmatta" title="J&#225;nos Harmatta">J&#225;nos Harmatta</a> concludes that the royal tribes and kings of the Xiongnu bore Iranian names, that all Xiongnu words noted by the Chinese can be explained from a <a href="/wiki/Scythian_languages" title="Scythian languages">Scythian language</a>, and that it is therefore clear that the majority of Hsiung-nu tribes spoke an Eastern Iranian language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmatta488_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harmatta488-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongolic_theories">Mongolic theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mongolic theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mongolic_languages" title="Mongolic languages">Mongolic languages</a></div>\n<p>Mongolian and other scholars have suggested that the Xiongnu spoke a language related to the <a href="/wiki/Mongolic_languages" title="Mongolic languages">Mongolic languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>  Mongolian archaeologists proposed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slab_Grave_Culture" title="Slab Grave Culture">Slab Grave Culture</a> people were the ancestors of the Xiongnu, and some scholars have suggested that the Xiongnu may have been the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tumen_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tumen-11">[11]</a></sup> According to the "<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Song" title="Book of Song">Book of Song</a>", (section Joujan), Joujan\'s (<a href="/wiki/Rouran_Khaganate" title="Rouran Khaganate">Rouran Khaganate</a>) alternative name was "<a href="/wiki/Tatar_confederation" title="Tatar confederation">Tatar</a>" or "Tartar" and they were a Xiongnu tribe". <a href="/wiki/Hyacinth_(Bichurin)" title="Hyacinth (Bichurin)">Nikita Bichurin</a> considered Xiongnu and <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> to be two subgroups (or <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasties</a>) but the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnicity" title="Ethnicity">ethnicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-info_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-info-74">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> refers to the time of Modu Chanyu as "the remote times of our Chanyu" in his letter to Daoist <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Chuji" title="Qiu Chuji">Qiu Chuji</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howorth_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howorth-75">[74]</a></sup> Sun and moon symbol of Xiongnu that discovered by archaeologists is similar to Mongolian <a href="/wiki/Soyombo_symbol" title="Soyombo symbol">Soyombo symbol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkic_theories">Turkic theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Turkic theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a></div>\n<p>Proponents of a Turkic language theory include <a href="/wiki/Edward_Harper_Parker" title="Edward Harper Parker">E.H. Parker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Abel-R%C3%A9musat" title="Jean-Pierre Abel-R&#233;musat">Jean-Pierre Abel-R&#233;musat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Klaproth" title="Julius Klaproth">Julius Klaproth</a>, Kurakichi Shiratori, <a href="/wiki/Gustaf_John_Ramstedt" title="Gustaf John Ramstedt">Gustaf John Ramstedt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annemarie_von_Gabain" title="Annemarie von Gabain">Annemarie von Gabain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Omeljan_Pritsak" title="Omeljan Pritsak">Omeljan Pritsak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup> Some sources say the ruling class was proto-Turkic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hucker_1975_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hucker_1975-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> while others suggest it was proto-<a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Hun</a>. <a href="/wiki/Craig_Benjamin" title="Craig Benjamin">Craig Benjamin</a> sees the Xiongnu as either proto-Turks or <a href="/wiki/Proto-Mongols" title="Proto-Mongols">proto-Mongols</a> who possibly spoke a language related to the <a href="/wiki/Dingling" title="Dingling">Dingling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both the 7th-century Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Northern_Dynasties" title="History of the Northern Dynasties">History of the Northern Dynasties</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Zhou" title="Book of Zhou">Book of Zhou</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> an inscription in the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">Sogdian language</a>, report the Turks to be a subgroup of the Huns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yeniseian_theories">Yeniseian theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Yeniseian theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Yeniseian_languages" title="Yeniseian languages">Yeniseian languages</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Lajos_Ligeti" title="Lajos Ligeti">Lajos Ligeti</a> was the first to suggest that the Xiongnu spoke a Yeniseian language. In the early 1960s <a href="/wiki/Edwin_G._Pulleyblank" title="Edwin G. Pulleyblank">Edwin Pulleyblank</a> was the first to expand upon this idea with credible evidence. In 2000, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vovin" title="Alexander Vovin">Alexander Vovin</a> reanalyzed Pulleyblank\'s argument and found further support for it by utilizing the most recent reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese_phonology" title="Old Chinese phonology">Old Chinese phonology</a> by Starostin and Baxter and a single Chinese transcription of a sentence in the language of the <a href="/wiki/Jie_people" title="Jie people">Jie people</a>, a member tribe of the Xiongnu Confederacy. Previous Turkic interpretations of the aforementioned sentence do not match the Chinese translation as precisely as using Yeniseian grammar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Pulleybank and  D. N. Keightley asserted that the Xiongnu titles "were originally Siberian words but were later borrowed by the Turkic and Mongolic peoples".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> The Xiongnu language gave to the later Turkic and Mongolian empires a number of important culture words including Turkish t&#228;ngri, Mongolian tenggeri, was originally the Xiongnu word for &#8220;heaven&#8221;, chengli (th&#225;&#720;&#331;-wr&#601;&#769;j). Titles such as tarqan and tegin and kaghan were also inherited from the Xiongnu language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_ethnicities">Multiple ethnicities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Multiple ethnicities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since the early 19th century, a number of Western scholars have proposed a connection between various language families or subfamilies and the language or languages of the Xiongnu. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Terrien_de_Lacouperie" title="Albert Terrien de Lacouperie">Albert Terrien de Lacouperie</a> considered them to be multi-component groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geng_2005_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geng_2005-17">[17]</a></sup> Many scholars believe the Xiongnu confederation was a mixture of different ethno-linguistic groups, and that their main language (as represented in the Chinese sources) and its relationships have not yet been satisfactorily determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Kim rejects "old racial theories or even ethnic affiliations" in favour of the "historical reality of these extensive, multiethnic, polyglot steppe empires".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Chinese sources link the <a href="/wiki/Tiele_people" title="Tiele people">Tiele people</a> and Ashina to the Xiongnu, not all <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a>. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Zhou" title="Book of Zhou">Book of Zhou</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Dynasties" title="History of Northern Dynasties">History of the Northern Dynasties</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Ashina_tribe" title="Ashina tribe">Ashina</a> clan was a component of the Xiongnu confederation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhou50_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou50-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Northern99_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Northern99-91">[90]</a></sup> but this connection is disputed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christian2_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christian2-92">[91]</a></sup> and according to the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Sui" title="Book of Sui">Book of Sui</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Tongdian" title="Tongdian">Tongdian</a></i>, they were "mixed nomads" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9B%9C" title="wikt:&#38620;">&#38620;</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%83%A1" title="wikt:&#32993;">&#32993;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">&#26434;&#32993;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">z&#225; h&#250;</span></i>) from <a href="/wiki/Pingliang" title="Pingliang">Pingliang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sui84_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sui84-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tong197_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tong197-94">[93]</a></sup> The Ashina and Tiele may have been separate ethnic groups who mixed with the Xiongnu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnic_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethnic-95">[94]</a></sup> Indeed, Chinese sources link many nomadic peoples (<i>hu</i>; see <i><a href="/wiki/Five_Barbarians" title="Five Barbarians">Wu Hu</a></i>) on their northern borders to the Xiongnu, just as Greco-Roman historiographers called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pannonian" title="Pannonian">Avars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> "<a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>". The Greek <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Name_of_Turkey" title="Name of Turkey">Tourkia</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">&#932;&#959;&#965;&#961;&#954;&#943;&#945;</span>) was used by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">emperor and scholar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_VII_Porphyrogenitus" title="Constantine VII Porphyrogenitus">Constantine VII Porphyrogenitus</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/De_Administrando_Imperio" title="De Administrando Imperio">De Administrando Imperio</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PrinzingSalamon1999_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-PrinzingSalamon1999-97">[96]</a></sup> though in his use, "Turks" always referred to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magyars" title="Magyars">Magyars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howorth2008_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howorth2008-98">[97]</a></sup>  Such archaizing was a common literary <i>topos</i>, and implied similar geographic origins and nomadic lifestyle but not direct filiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghurs</a> claimed descent from the Xiongnu (according to Chinese history <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weishu" title="Weishu">Weishu</a></i>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate" title="Uyghur Khaganate">Uyghur Khaganate</a> was descended from a Xiongnu ruler),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-golden_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-golden-100">[99]</a></sup> but many contemporary scholars do not consider the modern Uyghurs to be of direct linear descent from the old Uyghur Khaganate because modern <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">Uyghur language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Uyghur_language" title="Old Uyghur language">Old Uyghur languages</a> are different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tursun_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tursun-101">[100]</a></sup> Rather, they consider them to be descendants of a number of people, one of them the ancient Uyghurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xinjiang_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-xinjiang-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_isolate_theories">Language isolate theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Language isolate theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Turkologist <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Doerfer" title="Gerhard Doerfer">Gerhard Doerfer</a> has denied any possibility of a relationship between the Xiongnu language and any other known language and rejected in the strongest terms any connection with Turkic or Mongolian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology_and_genetics">Archaeology and genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Archaeology and genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2785" data-file-width="2007" decoding="async" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG/220px-BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG/330px-BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG/440px-BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BronzeManOrdos3-1stCenturyBCE.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze plaque of a man of the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_Plateau" title="Ordos Plateau">Ordos Plateau</a>, long held by the Xiongnu. 3&#8211;1st century BCE. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>. <a href="/wiki/Otto_J._Maenchen-Helfen" title="Otto J. Maenchen-Helfen">Otto J. Maenchen-Helfen</a> notes that the statuette displays <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasoid" title="Caucasoid">Caucasoid</a> features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maenchen-Helfen369_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maenchen-Helfen369-106">[105]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>(see <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_East_Asians" title="Genetic history of East Asians">Genetic history of East Asians</a>)\n</p><p>The original geographic location of the Xiongnu is disputed among steppe archaeologists. Since the 1960s, the geographic origin of the Xiongnu has attempted to be traced through an analysis of Early Iron Age burial constructions. No region has been proven to have mortuary practices that clearly match that of the Xiongnu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">Archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The political center of the Xiongnu state was in Mongolia and almost all of the Xiongnu kings were buried in Mongolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Kozlov" title="Pyotr Kozlov">Pyotr Kozlov</a>\'s excavations of the royal tombs at the <a href="/wiki/Noin-Ula_burial_site" title="Noin-Ula burial site">Noin-Ula burial site</a> in northern Mongolia that date to around the first century CE provided a glimpse into the lost world of the Xiongnu. Other archaeological sites have been unearthed in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> and elsewhere; they represent the Neolithic and historical periods of the Xiongnu\'s history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> Those included the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_culture" title="Ordos culture">Ordos culture</a>, many of them had been identified as the Xiongnu cultures. The region was occupied predominantly by peoples showing Mongoloid features, known from their skeletal remains and artifacts. Portraits found in the Noin-Ula excavations demonstrate other cultural evidences and influences, showing that Chinese and Xiongnu art have influenced each other mutually. Some of these embroidered portraits in the Noin-Ula <a href="/wiki/Kurgan" title="Kurgan">kurgans</a> also depict the Xiongnu with long braided hair with wide ribbons, which is seen to be identical with the <a href="/wiki/Ashina_tribe" title="Ashina tribe">Ashina clan</a> hair-style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Well-preserved bodies in Xiongnu and pre-Xiongnu tombs in the Mongolian Republic and southern Siberia show both Mongoloid and Caucasian features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> Analysis of skeletal remains from sites attributed to the Xiongnu provides an identification of dolichocephalic Mongoloid, ethnically distinct from neighboring populations in present-day Mongolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> Russian and Chinese anthropological and craniofacial studies show that the Xiongnu were physically very heterogenous, with six different population clusters showing different degrees of Mongoloid and Caucasoid physical traits. These clusters point to significant cross-regional migrations (both east to west and west to east) that likely started in the Neolithic period and continued to the medieval Mongolian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tumen_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tumen-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3300" data-file-width="2300" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg/150px-Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg/225px-Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg/300px-Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_Hunnu_bow_-_National_Museum_of_Mongolian_History.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Xiongnu bow</div></div></div>\n<p>Presently, there exist four fully excavated and well documented cemeteries: <a href="/wiki/Ivolga_archaeological_site" title="Ivolga archaeological site">Ivolga</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> Dyrestui,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Burkhan Tolgoi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> and Daodunzi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> Additionally thousands of tombs have been recorded in Transbaikalia and Mongolia. In addition to these, the Tamir 1 excavation site from a 2005 Silkroad Arkanghai Excavation Project is the only Xiongnu cemetery in Mongolia to be fully mapped in scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> Tamir 1 was located on Tamiryn Ulaan Khoshuu, a prominent granitic outcrop near other cemeteries of the Neolithic, Bronze Age, and Mongol periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purcell_20&#8211;31_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purcell_20&#8211;31-121">[120]</a></sup> Important finds at the site included a lacquer bowl, glass beads, and three <a href="/wiki/TLV_mirror" title="TLV mirror">TLV mirrors</a>. Archaeologists from this project believe that these artifacts paired with the general richness and size of the graves suggests that this cemetery was for more important or wealthy Xiongnu individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purcell_20&#8211;31_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Purcell_20&#8211;31-121">[120]</a></sup> The TLV mirrors are of particular interest. Three mirrors were acquired from three different graves at the site. The mirror found at feature 160 is believed to be a low-quality, local imitation of a Han mirror, while the whole mirror found at feature 100 and fragments of a mirror found at feature 109 are believed to belong to the classical TLV mirrors and date back to the Xin Dynasty or the early to middle Eastern Han period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lai_34&#8211;43_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lai_34&#8211;43-122">[121]</a></sup> The archaeologists have chosen to, for the most part, refrain from positing anything about Han-Xiongnu relations based on these particular mirrors. However, they were willing to mention the following: "There is no clear indication of the ethnicity of this tomb occupant, but in a similar brick-chambered tomb of late Eastern Han period at the same cemetery, archaeologists discovered a bronze seal with the official title that the Han government bestowed upon the leader of the Xiongnu. The excavators suggested that these brick chamber tombs all belong to the Xiongnu (Qinghai 1993)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lai_34&#8211;43_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lai_34&#8211;43-122">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Classifications of these burial sites make distinction between two prevailing type of burials: "(1). monumental ramped terrace tombs which are often flanked by smaller "satellite" burials and (2) \'circular\' or \'ring\' burials."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> Some scholars consider this a division between "elite" graves and "commoner" graves. Other scholars, find this division too simplistic and not evocative of a true distinction because it shows "ignorance of the nature of the mortuary investments and typically luxuriant burial assemblages [and does not account for] the discovery of other lesser interments that do not qualify as either of these types."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A majority (89%) of Xiongnu mitochondrial sequences from the Egyin Gol Valley can be classified as belonging to Asian <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup" title="Haplogroup">haplogroups</a>, and nearly 11% belong to European mitochondrial haplogroups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keyser-Tracqui_et_al_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keyser-Tracqui_et_al-125">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over the past decade, Chinese archaeologists have published several reviews regarding the results of excavations in Xinjiang. They imply the Xiongnu\'s supreme ruling class. Particularly interesting are the tombs in the cemetery at Heigouliang, Xinjiang (the Black Gouliang cemetery, also known as the summer palace of the Xiongnu king), east of the Barkol basin, near the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hami_City" title="Hami City">Hami</a>. By typing results of DNA samples during the excavation of one of the tombs, it was determined that of the 12 men: 6 Q1a* (not Q1a1-M120, not Q1a1b-M25, not Q1a2-M3), 4 Q1b-M378, 2 Q* (not Q1a, not Q1b: unable to determine subclades):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><ul><li>In a paper (Lihongjie 2012), the author analyzed the Y-DNAs of the ancient male samples from the 2nd or 1st century BCE cemetery at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Heigouliang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Heigouliang (page does not exist)">Heigouliang</a> in Xinjiang &#8211; which is also believed to be the site of a summer palace for Xiongnu kings &#8211; which is east of the Barkol basin and near the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hami_City" title="Hami City">Hami</a>. The Y-DNA of 12 men excavated from the site belonged to Q-MEH2 (Q1a) or Q-M378 (Q1b). The Q-M378 men among them were regarded as hosts of the tombs; half of the Q-MEH2 men appeared to be hosts and the other half as sacrificial victims.</li></ul></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_monster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2127" data-file-width="1936" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gold_monster.jpg/220px-Gold_monster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gold_monster.jpg/330px-Gold_monster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gold_monster.jpg/440px-Gold_monster.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_monster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold stag with eagle\'s head, and ten further heads in the antlers. From a Xiongnu tomb on the frontier, 4th&#8211;3rd century BC</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_distinctions">Artistic distinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Artistic distinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="box-One_source plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-one_source" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>relies largely or entirely on a single <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources">source</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Xiongnu##" title="Talk:Xiongnu">talk page</a>. Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by introducing <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">citations</a> to additional sources.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2012</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Within the Xiongnu culture more variety is visible from site to site than from "era" to "era," in terms of the Chinese chronology, yet all form a whole that is distinct from that of the Han and other peoples of the non-Chinese north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup> In some instances iconography can not be used as the main cultural identifier because art depicting animal predation is common among the steppe peoples. An example of animal predation associated with Xiongnu culture is a tiger carrying dead prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup> We see a similar image in work from Maoqinggou, a site which is presumed to have been under Xiongnu political control but is still clearly non-Xiongnu. From Maoqinggou, we see the prey replaced by an extension of the tiger\'s foot. The work also depicts a lower level of execution; Maoqinggou work was executed in a rounder, less detailed style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup> In its broadest sense, Xiongnu iconography of animal predation include examples such as the gold headdress from Aluchaideng and gold earrings with a turquoise and jade inlay discovered in Xigouban, Inner Mongolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup> The gold headdress can be viewed, along with some other examples of Xiongnu art, from the external links at the bottom of this article.\n</p><p>Xiongnu art is harder to distinguish from <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scythian_art" title="Scythian art">Scythian art</a>. There was a similarity present in stylistic execution, but Xiongnu art and Saka art did often differ in terms of iconography. Saka art does not appear to have included predation scenes, especially with dead prey, or same-animal combat. Additionally, Saka art included elements not common to Xiongnu iconography, such as a winged, horned horse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-4"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup> The two cultures also used two different bird heads. Xiongnu depictions of birds have a tendency to have a moderate eye and beak and have ears, while Saka birds have a pronounced eye and beak and no ears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-5"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>102&#8211;103</span></sup> Some scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2014)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> claim these differences are indicative of cultural differences. Scholar Sophia-Karin Psarras claims that Xiongnu images of animal predation, specifically tiger plus prey, is spiritual, representative of death and rebirth, and same-animal combat is representative of the acquisition of or maintenance of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psarras_127-6"><a href="#cite_note-Psarras-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>102&#8211;103</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rock_art_and_writing">Rock art and writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Rock art and writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The rock art of the <a href="/wiki/Yin_Mountains" title="Yin Mountains">Yin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helan_Mountains" title="Helan Mountains">Helan Mountains</a> is dated from the 9th millennium BC to the 19th century AD. It consists mainly of engraved signs (petroglyphs) and only minimally of painted images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Excavations conducted between 1924 and 1925 in the Noin-Ula kurgans produced objects with over twenty carved characters, which were either identical or very similar to that of to the runic letters of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_alphabet" title="Old Turkic alphabet">Old Turkic alphabet</a> discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Orkhon_Valley" title="Orkhon Valley">Orkhon Valley</a>. From this a some scholars hold that the Xiongnu had a script similar to Eurasian runiform and this alphabet itself served as the basis for the ancient Turkic writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> (vol. <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/s:%E5%8F%B2%E8%A8%98/%E5%8D%B7110" title="zh:s:&#21490;&#35352;/&#21367;110">110</a>) state that when the Xiongnu noted down something or transmitted a message, they made cuts on a piece of wood; they also mention a "Hu script".\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif/70px-Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif/105px-Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif/140px-Fig6Ishjamts_p166R1.gif 2x" width="70"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>2nd century BCE &#8211; 2nd century CE characters of Hun-<a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> script (Mongolia and Inner Mongolia), N. Ishjamts, "Nomads In Eastern Central Asia", in the <i>History of civilizations of Central Asia</i>, Volume 2, Fig 5, p.&#160;166, UNESCO Publishing, 1996, <style CE, characters of Hun-Xianbei script (Mongolia and Inner Mongolia), N. Ishjamts, "Nomads In Eastern Central Asia", in the <i>History of civilizations of Central Asia</i>, Volume 2, Fig 5, p.&#160;166, UNESCO Publishing, 1996, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/92-3-102846-4" title="Special:BookSources/92-3-102846-4">92-3-102846-4</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Xiongnu were a nomadic people. From their lifestyle of herding flocks and their horse-trade with China, we can conclude that their diet consist mainly of <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">mutton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horse meat</a> and wild geese that were shot down.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_connection_to_Silla_Dynasty">Possible connection to Silla Dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Possible connection to Silla Dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In various kinds of ancient inscriptions on monuments of <a href="/wiki/Munmu_of_Silla" title="Munmu of Silla">Munmu of Silla</a>, it is recorded that King <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> came from Xiongnu. Also, there are some Korean researchers point out grave goods of Silla and Xiongnu are alike and also some researchers propose that Silla King is descended from Xiongnu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> The Korean traditional cloth <a href="/wiki/Hanbok" title="Hanbok">Hanbok</a> shows some <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mei.edu_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-mei.edu-136">[135]</a></sup> The royal <a href="/wiki/Crowns_of_Silla" title="Crowns of Silla">Crowns of Silla</a> are identical to <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Persian Crowns</a> found in today Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mei.edu_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-mei.edu-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> About this, the Korean public broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Korean_Broadcasting_System" title="Korean Broadcasting System">KBS</a> has reported a documentary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chanyu#List_of_Xiongnu_Chanyus" title="Chanyu">List of Xiongnu rulers (Chanyus)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chanyu#Chanyu_family_trees" title="Chanyu">Rulers family tree</a></li>\n<li><a history</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_Dynasty" title="History of the Han Dynasty">History of the Han Dynasty</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ban_Yong" title="Ban Yong">Ban Yong</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Zubu" title="Zubu">Zubu</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This view was put forward to <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a> in AD 14<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xiongnu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Zheng Zhang (Chinese: &#37165;&#24373;), Shang-fang (Chinese: &#23578;&#33459;). <a class="external text"
